I would be apt to point out that the 70's remains their high water mark, but one can't deny that the 80's revealed a more \\"fun\\" version of the Judas Priest sound.  They became more adept at writing radio hits that were still viable enough to be enjoyed by their traditional fan base.  \\"Rapid Fire\\" is a catchy proto-speed metal track that opens the album perfectly and segues into \\"Metal Gods\\", a track that remains a metal anthem decades later.  The two radio hits \\"Living After Midnight\\" and \\"Breaking The Law\\" are great \\"hooks in the brain\\" type songs, even if they do become tedious soon after you become acquainted with them.  The real gems on this album for me are \\"United\\" and \\"The Rage\\".  The former is an extremely catchy arena rock anthem, and the latter is the most deliciously hard rocking bastard on the entire album.  When the dust settles one could not claim that this album is on equal footing with what Priest accomplished in the 70's, nor does it stand on equal footing with the best 80's offering (Defenders Of The Faith).  Instead it remains a great introduction to the band due to it's dual nature of radio ready anthems, and hard rocking tunes.

B0038ZITIE|4.0|I haven't seen this film since I was girl but watched it again the other day for the first time in thirty years and thought it great fun! Yes, it's all a bit silly with trite dialogue, zero characterisation and a decidedly holey plot - but that's to miss the point. This is a movie to be enjoyed for its sheer exuberance not held up to detailed critical analysis.    So we have that fab Queen song (the rest of their soundtrack ain't bad either). A cast that lurches from the so-bad-they're-a-joy-to-watch (ie Sam J Jones and Melody Anderson) to some unexpectedly good (in a camp way) turns from actors who should really know better (Topol, Dalton & Blessed)... and what they hell was Max von Sydow doing in this? Had he upset Bergman that year? If so the Swede's loss is our gain as he's dastardly good as Ming. Then there are so oddly delicious roles - such as Klytus and General Kala. The look of the film is quasi art deco with a really gaudy use of colour that defies all expectation to work. There are some decent set-pieces (the football fight, attack on Rocket Ajax) and some naff special effects. There are also a handful of unexpected gags. The princess's pet, for instance, is called 'Fellini'!    One thing that took my by surprise - and which obviously I didn't pick up on as a kid - is how pervy it all is. There's definitely a kinky overtone to the movie: from Ming's 'orgasmatron' ring to Flash in his leather pants and the obvious sadistic joy of Klytus whipping Orla etc.    Finally, although this film was made in the wake of 'Star Wars' in the late 70s, I wonder if its real influence is 'The Wizard of Oz'. Beyond its bright colours, there are loads of references - such as the hourglass in the prison, the troops that look like flying-monkeys, Kala (the witch) melting at the end.     Like I say, this film is great fun. It may not be Bergman (to mention him again) but what would you rather watch on a rainy Sunday afternoon?

B000002L7R|5.0|It is a very well-known fact that Prince Rogers Nelson is a musical genius. And yet, fans, in 1985, were quite surprised when he introduced this album to the world. While gravitating gracefully from his pre-Purple Rain success, Prince secretly went to work on his seventh studio album. In addition to choosing low-key publicity for this follow-up project, Mr. Nelson preferred to keep the entire album under wraps right up until the very first airing of the music video for &#34;Raspberry Beret.&#34; Mr. Purple Passion was clearly functioning on a new level with this album, not just personally, but in music as a whole. The ambiance of this album, with it's Middle Eastern inspired, neo-psychedelic abstraction, sees Prince further diversifying musically, adding orchestrations to his music, and presenting an overall Mediterranean feel. Ultimately, when &#34;Around the World in a Day&#34; made it's premier in April of '85, it shower down from the clouds in complete victory, going on to double platinum success. I own numerous albums by Prince (and the Revolution), so I will stateif you are looking for a replica of &#34;1999&#34; or &#34;Purple Rain,&#34; best better look elsewhere. Because what you have here is a palate of rarities, a culmination of Prince's genius; the pinnacle of an artist whose talent was unrivaled by all whom derived from the '80s Pop/R&B/Funk/New Wave era! Furthermore, If you're an eternal fan such as myself, then most likely you already own this album. If for some insane reason that you are in fact not a fan, then there's a good chance that you will miss your ticket to a day of true cultural enthrallment!<br /><br />Track listing for Around the World in a Day:<br /><br />1. Around the World in a Day (3:28)<br /><br />2. Paisley Park (4:42)<br /><br />3. Condition of the Heart (6:48)<br /><br />4. Raspberry Beret (3:32)<br /><br />5. Tambourine (2:47)<br /><br />6. America (3:42)<br /><br />7. Pop Life (3:43)<br /><br />8. The Ladder (5:29)<br /><br />9. Temptation (8:17)<br /><br />All caution was challenged head-on for this album, as evident by the incorporation of instruments such as the cello, the violin, the finger cymbals, the electric bass and the drums. Upon hearing the tribal opening for the first track &#34;Around the World in a Day,&#34; one might assume that Prince returned home from a long overdue vacation from the West Indies. The interlace of Chinese and Egyptian culture is heavily utilized. Next, the carnivalesque &#34;Paisley Park,&#34; which shares its name with Prince's recording complex Paisley Park Studios, and tells of a Utopian place one can go to escape the chaos of the world around them. The echo of the rock-guitar and the 3-point pounding beat leaves plenty of space for the occasional clinging finger cymbals. And what would a signature recording be without the glorious backing vocals provided by Wendy Melvoin and Lisa Coleman? Both artists' vocals radiate! Moving forward, making it's mellow entrance, the exquisite &#34;Condition of the Heart.&#34; I love every second of this beautiful ballad. Prince doesn't actually start singing until 2:38, thus leaving a great stretch of time to display definitive piano work. This track is one of Mr. Nelson's most flawless, effortless ballads, ever!<br /><br />Things get more upbeat with the affectionate aura of &#34;Raspberry Beret,&#34; which opens with a pattern of sharp hand claps, complete with a 1-3 countdown. Thereafter begins this global celebration of stringed instruments plucking and tuning from start to finish, including the clinging of arabesque finger cymbals, gentle strums of the harp, and a great deal of the violin, all tied together with a bouncy bottom-heavy, and might I add, very catchy, bassline. The music video for its time was pure genius, exhibiting an array of the human race, further reflecting the worldly branding of this album. In the video, Prince arrives to entertain his lady love and the commons. Styling from head to toe, Prince performs with grandeur perfection, while donning a 2-piece, cloud print, sky blue suit, a snug white ruffled frock, and sporting a short dark sleek hair-do. And we must not forget the appearance of his white &#34;Purple Rain&#34; guitar! The intro used for the album differs from the music video version (my favorite), and can be found on the &#34;Ultimate&#34; album in 12&#34; format! &#34;Tambourine&#34; has a distinctively form-fitting sound of 1985, particularly the percussion. The lyrics come across as sexually suggestive, but quickly dissolve due to the exhilaration of the up-beat rhythm. &#34;America,&#34; a song literally being torn apart by the opposing forces of Rock & Roll and Funk, brings about a hardcore resonance. The tempestuous patriarch presents his outlook on the hardships of this country, clamoring the truth with such serious meaning. The power-driven anthem explodes with an enraged rock-guitar, puttering percussion, and the tambourine, all collectively paving the way for this momentous occasion...entertaining the hearts of the caring and compassionate...far and wide...from seeeea toooo shiiiiiniiiiing seeeea!<br /><br />The rhythmic &#34;Pop Life&#34; slowly begins with a faded-in synth line and quickly releases smooth bass guitar and piano embellishments. The drum machine provides occasional handclaps, constructing more of a danceable beat. Aside from the female empowerment of Wendy and Lisa, Ms. Sheila E. makes a guest appearance on the drums. Prince sails the entire barrage with insight on the greed of '80s Pop Culture, taunting: &#34;What's the matter with your life? Is the poverty bringing you down? Is the mailman jerking you 'round? Did he put your million dollar check in someone else's box?&#34;...a message that, in my opinion, rings true in the millennial era! &#34;The Ladder&#34; is yet another lovely ballad entry and welcomes in the sensual vibrations of the saxophone. The beat mirrors a slow-tempo rhythm that is systematically sluggish, and everything is held together with a choir of all-star background vocals, including Susannah, Tai and, of course, the talented Wendy and Lisa. Lastly, the hot and heavy, pulsating &#34;Temptation,&#34; provided with the most insistently catchy sax line ever put on a Pop record. The heat rises to the extreme within a minute in, forcing the overall mood to implode, consequently igniting the passion in Princes' guttural screams! The end section to this song is securely intertwined with an unwinding piano and the audible struggle between love and lust. Every song listed succeeds its purpose; there's simply not one bad recording to be found here! ATWIAD is just a taste for those who are &#34;beginners&#34;, and will most likely leave you longing for his additional material, some of which can be found on several greatest hits compilations.<br /><br />This album depicts Mr. Purple Passion in his prime, exploring his talent in ways few could've predicted. In my humble opinion, this is one of Prince's greatests recordings! So what exactly is it that makes &#34;Around the World in a Day&#34; so pleasureful? What makes it the indisputable standout debut of ANY Prince album? Well, for me, I think it has a lot to do with the worldly resonance presented. You have to remember, this was the post We-Are-the-World era in music; an era when musicians were joining forces and singing for a heartfelt cause; an era when creating music was accomplished with true meaning! One might say this album was Prince's cultural contribution to the world at that time in history. Personally, I feel that Mr. Nelson had a purpose when developing the foundation for this masterpiece. The production was above and beyond anything that came before it. Every instrument used on this album was more polished than ever before, which is evident in every single note written and performed. Prince's fashionable entourage, The Revolution, had a huge influence on the aesthetic of this album as well, particularlyfrom the contribution of Bobby Z (whom I absolutely adore), Brownmark, Wendy Melvoin, Lisa Coleman and Matt &#34;Doctor&#34; Fink - all whom played a significant role in the worldwide success within the group! Modifications I made:  I used a knife sharpening stone to round out the edges of clamping end of the \\"handle/tongs\\" so they won't scrape against the soft bowl and non-stick finish.    First, I recommend against using Isopropyl (rubbing) alcohol as it will left a nasty ash that is difficult to clean and will stain anything it touches.  The fuels I have tried are 70% ethyl rubbing alcohol (in yellow labels) at RiteAid or most drug stores, or denatured alcohol you can get a WalMart or any hardware store.  With the denatured alcohol, I would dilute it very slightly (1 part water with 9 parts denatured alcohol) because pure denatured alcohol burns too high.  With the 70% ethyl rubbing alcohol, no diluting is needed.

Jewel is more concerned about escape and can't understand why Blu tolerates humans. She initially rescued him before he learned how to fly and it becomes clear that he still hasn't discovered how it's done. Blu is aided by a variety of other birds as he attempts to find Linda. The gang enlists minions to find Blu and Jewel, resulting in a number of fights and chase scenes.<br /><br />Rio lacks the heart of Pixar movies, but it does have some redeeming qualities. The story is far from original and it's not difficult to guess the entire plot after a few minutes. Despite that, some of the scenes are quite charming. It's fast-paced and doesn't contain any dull moments. I imagine younger children being captivated by Blu's adventures and the color scheme and ever-present Brazilian music is more than enough to hold their attention.<br /><br />I enjoyed the story. Yes, I knew what was going to happen, but I found the characters quite likable. The animation becomes more detailed as the story progresses and some of the scenes contain incredible detail. B004Y1USV2|4.0|Ok...there are sort of 2 parts to this review: the subjective part (\\"this sucks\\" or \\"this rocks\\") which is usually smaller and not-so-qualified, and the comparison to previous Yes efforts that might make it more relevant to the average reader out there (\\"hmmm, he said it's more like 'Drama' than 'Talk' and I hated 'Drama').  Some folks don't seem to make that distinction much, merely pronouncing a work as \\"crap\\" or a \\"masterpiece\\" with little reasoning.     First off, to my decently-trained (though mostly self-trained) prog-proficient musical ears, \\"Fly from Here\\" sounds most like \\"Drama\\" from the Yes catalogue.  Not surprising to most reading this as it's a common theme in most of the reviews here.  Having a center-piece song that was partly written during the Drama-era by a large chunk of the Drama lineup and finally finished by almost that same lineup has (not surprisingly) led to a fairly Drama-ish sounding tune.  The fact that Trevor Horn (vocalist from the Drama lineup) produced \\"Fly from Here\\" (the entire CD, not just the big track) is kind of the final glob of hot wax in sealing the very-Drama-like final product.  Lastly, David Benoit (or is it Benoit David?), the \\"new guy on vox\\" replacement on this CD is kind of a vocal chameleon.  He sort of sounds like Jon Anderson in some parts and then like Trevor Horn in different parts of the CD as well as sounding like neither in a couple places.  And I first heard him singing for a band called \\"Mystery\\" where he frequently sounded like Dennis DeYoung (singer/keyboard guy from the classic Styx lineup) so go figure.  He has some flexible vocal chords.    And unlike many Yes-nut-purists who think \\"Close to the Edge\\" was something close to unworldly in its awesomeness (which I about 95% agree with) but \\"Drama\\" was bogus, I've always really liked \\"Drama\\".  Howe's legato guitar work on the disc was a big draw as well as Downes' adding some nice keyboard stylings and colors different from Wakeman.  Squire's bass on \\"Drama\\" was still very much sounding like Chris Squire's bass, and \\"Drama\\" was the disc that showed me how much Squire's voice shapes the overall sound of Yes' vocals.      So for me, the fact this disc reminds me of Drama is a good thing.      Production on this is very slick, and given Trevor Horn's track record as a producer, it isn't too surprising.  BUT...for me, it's a little too polished in places (that's one of those subjective assessments).  There's a LOT of padding synths and strummed acoustic guitar, which isn't a problem, but it's definitely not something I'm used to in a lot of Yes tunes.  Intricately picked, complicated solos and runs, and melodic execution on an acoustic guitar frequently have graced Yes albums, but just plain strumming, not so much.  And somewhat similar to my review of Asia \\"Phoenix\\", here's an album that also sounds like it came from the same guys from the same band, but understandably, a little less aggressively than in most of their old albums (including \\"Drama\\") because they ARE a little bit older and less aggressive.    It's a shame the core Yes guys aren't capable of writing and performing stuff on par with \\"Close to the Edge\\" or \\"Going for the One\\" (on par to my ear anyway), but as no one has learned how to escape growing older, changing, and dying (other than by dying young), it's something I've learned to live with.  For me this is an enjoyable album, albeit nothing fabulous.  To be honest, I've been getting into Glass Hammer's \\"If\\" which seems to have captured a much more \\"classic Yes\\" vibe than this disk, and it also has a Jon Anderson sound-alike vocalist (what is it with so many prog bands who are scared to just field an original sounding vocalist??).    Three and half stars with a roundup to four.

B00006YXOX|5.0|I waited several years to write this review, not knowing how I could articulate how much these symphonies have meant to me.  I never would have expected that the usually overblown style of Wagner could be used in such a devout way.  But more surprising to me was that his symphonies were often structured with the big picture--the whole symphony taken as a whole--in mind.  When listening to him, each movement feels part of something larger, for there is usually a climactic point in his symphonies, which was a totally new thing for me to experience.  I think that Bruckner's concept of a higher, spiritual purpose to music beyond classical formality, musical contrast, thematic development, or emotional expression has impacted me hugely.  It takes some getting used to his style--it's not dissonant, just more spread-out and unpredictable than Schubert, Schumann, or Mendelssohn (Beethoven is questionable)--but even so, there is so much to appreciate in what Bruckner did that the sometimes annoying detours in his movements hardly matter.  If there's only one movement you listen to, listen to the 2nd movement of the 7th symphony.  But if you want to experience him much more fully, listen to all of his symphonies.  This is the CD set I got and has excellent booklet notes.

B0039PPW60|4.0|I've been using the X1204USB mixer for a few weeks now, and I must say I am extremely pleased with the product.  This has enough options/features that is certainly takes a bit of time to get a feel for everything this product has to offer.    Key items:  USB connectivity - The sound quality that comes across from the USB connection is superb.  I'm not sure why so many reviews talk about having a hard time setting this up (I do admit that I ignored the drivers that came with the mixer and got the latest drivers from the Behringer website).  There is a button controlling the USB feed into the main mix (the buttons controlling the USB signal flow are the red ones on the right, below the effects generator).  In my 'I don't want to read the manual haste', all I did was plug in the mixer, hooked up a guitar and the USB, once it connected to the PC, started playing the guitar and flipping switches until I got signal to come out on the PC.  If you want to hear playback from the PC back to the mixer while you are playing, make sure you select that signal feed in the monitor group (you have 3 options, Main Mix, Aux 3+4, and 2TR/USB).    Effects Generator:  good quality, I see this being used mostly for live performances in my case as you can layer in the same or better effects with you DAW and might as well record a clean signal so you can 'futz' with it later.  Even so, the reverb and chorus sound pretty solid.    Documentation:  This is one of the main reasons this didn't get 5 stars...  The documentation provided is lacking, but there is a comprehensive manual on the website.    Included Software:  Second reason this didn't get 5 stars.  The energyXT2.5 Compact Behringer Edition DAW that comes with the mixer is relatively useless.  By 'compact', they mean 'we will show you about 5% of what this can do, but continually recommend you drop the cash for the full version'.  If you aren't ready to drop for a high end DAW like Pro Tools, then stick with the open source Audacity (also included) and use the VST plug ins that come with the CD.      All in all, I am very pleased with this purchase.  Amazon had it for about $40 cheaper that Guitar Center (and Prime eligible, which was key).  I 100% recommend a beginner using this item as their entry into digital recording, as well as a solid choice for any small group that wants to perform at the local bar.

Whether you believe it or not chances are that many would've had the same mindset and make the same choices had we been in Jaycee's situation at the time.      As for the book, I can't believe the amount of ridiculous complaints on here about how she wrote this.  Maybe it's because that this is the first memoir/autobiography I've ever read possibly ever, but it's a really impressive bookand I was rather surprised she was able to get this written and published so quickly.  I thought it would be a whole decade before we would see a book like this.  And even though she said that she might tell more about her story in the future (when she has a better understanding of it), I learned a lot more about what happened to her in this book than all the mixed of up info the media has provided since the story came out in 2009.  She even corrected some of the media's errors about certain details.  As far as memoirs go, I know most of them are ghostwritten, so give her some credit.  She wrote the whole damn thing herself which is more than I can say for other media icons who could easily fabricate what they want their readers to read.  I was a Creative Writing major in college and believe me when I say that the courses didn't teach me a whole lot about writing than what I've learned through my own constant practice of writing the stuff I wanted to write.  I think it's a great start and I know in time, she'll get so much better at it and may one day become a professional.  Despite all the bad that the whole world knows happens to her, the book ends as a feel good story which is in essence the way good books should be.  So please buy and read this book if you haven't already.

0141439688|4.0|SLIGHT SPOILERS<br /><br />So, Persuasion.  Austen has an uncanny ability to create characters who are so petty and unlikable that one simply wants to put the book down, for lack of someone with sense.  This problem, for me, brings Northanger Abbey to its knees, and weighs down Persuasion for the first seventy pages.  It's a while before we get to be inside Anne Elliot's head, and in the meantime we must satisfy ourselves with her imbecilic, preening father and his protegee daughter, Elizabeth.  But finally, we get to Anne Elliot and Captain Wentworth, to marriage intrigue and the questioning of glances and motives--all of the things for which one absolutely loves Austen.  This book provides the goods, so don't give up on it too early.<br /><br />Persuasion is it's own creature, however.  I was immediately struck by the difference in style from her earlier book Pride and Prejudice; in Persuasion, Austen strings together dependent clauses as though the story desperately required the reader to be in suspense as to what verb she will next employ.  Take, for instance, the reader's first impression of Bath:<br /><br />  \\"They were come too late in the year for any amusement or variety which Lyme, as a public place, might offer; the rooms were shut up, the lodgers almost all gone, scarcely any family but of the residents left; and, as there is nothing to admire in the buildings themselves, the remarkable situation of the town, the principal street almost hurrying into the water, the walk to the Cobb, skirting round the pleasant little bay, which, in the season, is animated with bathing machines and company; the Cobb itself, its old wonders and new improvements, with the very beautiful line of cliffs stretching out to the east of the town, are what the stranger's eye will seek; and a very strange stranger it must be, who does not see charms in the immediate environs of Lyme, to make him wish to know it better.\\"<br /><br />Some will take pleasure in reading these lines, but I personally think they do her no credit.  At its best, her prose style is lucid, and suffused with that clean, English wit we expect.  That being said, I find Austen more charming than aesthetically stunning, and to my tastes, her simplicity of style adds to that charm.  Her convolutions detract from it.<br /><br />This issue was exacerbated by the decision of the editor, Gillian Beer, to not standardize punctuation.  Austen has a tendency to interrupt an otherwise complete clause with a comma, which is off-putting to modern readers and detracts from her otherwise fluid style.  Likewise, there has been no attempt to  regulate her use of the semicolon, which 19th century authors used with abandon.  Beer might argue that these editorial decisions might affect interpretation, but I never noticed a moment when emphasis might depend upon an otherwise superfluous comma.  Furthermore, if there had been any instance where punctuation greatly affected meaning, I think Beer would have mentioned it in her notes--she was so very thorough.  Incidentally, this lack of editorial judiciousness brings me to my final contention with the edition.<br /><br />While there are different opinions as to the proper domain of footnotes, I believe they exist for only a few purposes.  1.)  They should explain historical and cultural references that may otherwise be outside the reader's ken.  2.)  They should explain a historical use of a word that a modern reader may otherwise not know.  3.)  They should give manuscript variations, when applicable.  They should not (in my opinion) explain turns of phrase, or help the reader interpret the plot, or offer critiques of the novel.  My reasoning is that these three tasks belong to the reader; for an editor to do all the heavy lifting actually detracts from the pleasure of reading the book.  For instance, when Mr. Elliot implies that he would like to marry Anne, he says,<br /><br />  \\"The name of Anne Elliot,\\" said he, \\"has long had an interesting sound to me.  Very long has it possessed a charm over my fancy; and, if I dared, I would breathe my wishes that the name might never change.\\"<br /><br />To this excellent, shining line of dialogue, Beer graciously provides a footnote:  \\"An oblique proposal: her name and his are the same, Elliot.\\"  This is completely unnecessary, and for me mars the beauty of the original sentence.  Also, it is inconceivable that the reader might not yet know that both Anne and Mr. Elliot share the same last name, as this exchange occurs late in the book.  Unfortunately, this is not the only stupid footnote in the Penguin edition.  For the sake of space, I will refrain from listing any others.<br /><br />On the whole, this is a fine novel; not as accomplished as some of Austen's other work, but very engaging and fun to read.  She seems to be a bit more moralistic as here well, which is not to my taste, but may be to others.  Oh that Mr. Elliot and his \\"Sunday travelling\\"--ought he not to be in church?  My only complaint with the novel itself (other than the occasional tortuous/torturous sentence) is the speedy resolution, which I felt could have benefited from a couple more scenes concerning the novel's two main antagonists--a confrontation would have been nice here.  Also, I felt Austen took the easy way out by not forcing Anne to make a choice between two eligible suitors.  Otherwise, if you like Austen, this is probably exactly what you're looking for.I dared, I would breathe my wishes that the name might never change.\\"  To this excellent, shining line of dialogue, Beer graciously provides a footnote:  \\"An oblique proposal: her name and his are the same, Elliot.\\"  This is completely unnecessary, and for me mars the beauty of the original sentence.  Also, it is inconceivable that the reader might not yet know that both Anne and Mr. Elliot share the same last name, as this exchange occurs late in the book.  Unfortunately, this is not the only stupid footnote in the Penguin edition.  For the sake of space, I will refrain from listing any others.  On the whole, this is a fine novel; not as accomplished as some of Austen's other work, but very engaging and fun to read. My only complaint with the novel itself (other than the occasional tortuous/torturous sentence) is the speedy resolution, which I felt could have benefited from a couple more scenes concerning the novel's two main antagonists--a confrontation would have been nice here.  Also, I felt Austen took the easy way out by not forcing Anne to make a choice between two eligible suitors.  Otherwise, if you like Austen, this is probably exactly what you're looking for.

0553801473|1.0|Martin's ASOIAF displayed a distinctive, original style from the get-go, and before this series started to bog down in book 4, I was impressed as well by the author's vitality. Here was a writer who had \\"fire in the belly,\\" and I was happy to be carried along with the project.<br /><br />Unfortunately, book 5 is not a return to form, but rather a complete debacle. Am I saddened that this series is going nowhere? Yes. It all once seemed so promising. Am I saddened that Martin has apparently lost his enthusiasm for the whole project? Yes. Martin once seemed so inspired.<br /><br />However, I am more angered than saddened by this lackluster effort, because A Dance With Dragons strikes me not just as a bad book, but a DISHONEST one as well. This is why I give the book only one star. Martin doesn't really have a story to tell, but he decides to tell one anyway. He thus stalls for nearly a thousand pages, throwing in some important occurrences near the end that are vague or undeveloped enough as to mean possibly anything. His cliffhanger endings thus provide an uneventful and tedious book with a semblance of direction, while simultaneously buying Martin many more years to figure out where it is he wants to take the story. (A concrete example: what really happened at the wall? Obviously something important, but what exactly? Details are sketchy, and many plausible alternatives exist.  Hopefully in Winds of Winter we will learn what this plot advancement of ADWD actually entailed!!) Martin's readers do not deserve to be taken advantage of like this.<br /><br />A contributing factor to the demise of this series seems to be an unhealthy obsession with world building. Martin's world building has obviously enriched ASOIAF, but it has created plot problems, and it has also-especially in ADWD-been utilized as a way to fill space in the absence of plot development. And perhaps it has left Martin too exhausted to resolve the very problems that his ever expanding story has created. \\"My fantasy world is bigger than yours\\" is George RR Martin's response/retort to Tolkien, the fantasy Godfather that Martin wants to replace. (The insistence on RR in the name can hardly be coincidental.) Martin is not the only fantasy writer who has run into problems with the \\"more is better\\" philosophy, but his series does seem to be collapsing under its own weight. World building can lead to monumental art, but if taken to extremes it can also result in monumental folly. Martin's reach may have exceeded his grasp, and his ambition ironically may be responsible for his seeming inability to advance the story. What a convoluted mess ASOIAF has become!<br /><br />A related problem: Fantasy writers I have cherished (Tolkien, Lewis, LeGuin, Donaldson) have written works that provide nourishment to the reader. (Sort of like lembas bread on a long journey.) As ASOIAF slows down, Martin resorts to ever increasing doses of sex, sexual violence, torture, humiliation etc. to keep the reader interested. Such \\"adult\\" material has of course always been a distinctive aspect of ASOIAF, but it is becoming too pervasive and gratuitous. None of this is any worse than much of mainstream media entertainment, but I do think, sadly, that Martin's bleak medieval freakfest has become more enervating than fortifying.<br /><br />I don't think this series will ever be completed, at least not by Martin. Since it hardly seems salvageable,and since Martin doesn't seem to have his heart in it anymore, maybe this is for the best. Despite it all, though, I still am glad I read the first 3 of the series. Unfortunately, the song ended there. Martin in his 2011 incarnation is something much more depressing to think about.\\"My fantasy world is bigger than yours\\" is George RR Martin's response/retort to Tolkien, the fantasy Godfather that Martin wants to replace. This book is about a heroine named Katniss Everdeen, who voluntarily replaces her sister to participate in a slaughterhouse festivity called the Hunger Games, hosted by the evil central government that is much like the government imagined in George Orwell's 1984. In the Hunger Games, two contestants (called tributes) from each of the 12 districts in the fictitious country of Panem (existing on the site of what used to be North America) are chosen through a lottery, and entered into an arena where they cannot escape unless they kill each other until only one of them remains as the sole survivor. As you can imagine, much of the book is spent describing the intensity of the battles that ensue between the tributes. However, the parts that drew me the most were the personal moments between the characters, particularly between Cinna and Katniss. Yes, all the kisses between Peeta and Katniss make for good a teenage entertainment, but beyond that, the characters actually seem to possess a depth that is beyond what is normally entailed in a young adult book. In its literary worth, the book straddles somewhere between The Giver and 1984, but in its pace and fun, I would place it right along the lines of Twilight if not higher.

Dolarhyde has his share of character flaws, and apparently so does Lonergan. Craig shares the spotlight quite well with Ford, who is a legendary Hollywood action star.<br /><br />Cowboys & Aliens` plot is summed up by its title. As I try and put into writing why cowboys are fighting aliens the silliness of it all becomes apparent, but more is the fool anyone going to a movie with the words cowboys and aliens in the title expecting the next Memento. The cliff notes, and spoiler free version is that a hostile alien race is kidnapping humans ahead of their planned, full scaled invasion of earth. The aliens possess vastly superior technology to the barely industrialized Americans of the latter half of the nineteenth century. This simple set up sets the stage for some great action and a few nice plot twists. The movie never gets too bogged down in character development. We get just enough of Jake Lonergan's past via flash back's so that we can see his character's evolution from anti-hero to plain hero. 0316066524|3.0|Potential readers should be warned:  Although Infinite Jest runs ~484,000 words, that evidently wasn't enough for author Wallace to provide readers with any resolution to his multiple, partially interlocking story-lines.  If that will bother you (as it did me and other readers), you probably shouldn't start this.  On the other hand its supporters will tell you \\"it's not the destination, it's the journey.\\"  And the novel is undeniably shot through with interesting characters, sub-plots, anecdotes and allusions.  At times I found it quite entertaining.  Hence the three-star rating.  Still, I have a strong 'classical' preference for Unity with my Variety, and that's just not what Wallace has on offer.<br /><br />What I mostly take away from reading this book is a portrait of the seriously disturbed, manic/depressive, substance-abusing, suicidal author.  This is, in other words, not a story at all...it's a chronicle of a man's mental condition as he attempts to write a story (ultimately failing).  Mr. Wallace committed suicide while in the depression end of his cycle; in his manic phase he penned Infinite Jest.  There is a constant pall-mall rush to his flow of run-on paragraphs, along with a literally compulsive listing of things and naming of irrelevant persons or events, that will drive many readers to \\"howling fantods\\".  More than that though:  His imaginings are so bizarre they more resemble the ravings of a person in delirium than genuinely creative outflow of a Nietzsche or Van Gogh. So much mutation, mutilation, addiction and insanity... so little beauty, wisdom or goodness.<br /><br />  Some  have promoted this book as funny, or a black comedy, or even \\"hilarious\\".  Not even!  Catch-22 and Dr. Strangelove (the movie) were black comedies, and they were hilarious.  Voltaire's Candide was at least amusing.  Infinite Jest virtually never tempted me to laugh or even smile.  Grimace sometimes, yes.<br /><br />  It's unfortunate, and a bad sign of our times, that some effete literary critics have hailed this style as \\"the next thing in fiction.\\" I'm convinced they're simply trying to be the first critic to have caught the New Wave.  This reminds me so much of the situation in modern art, the way pretentious critics contest with one another to identify some new trend or artist...while the art itself they're promoting is mostly just crap.  At any rate, 15 years since this book was published it doesn't appear that the David Foster Wallace style has caught on in any pervasive way. If it ever does, I'm through with contemporary fiction. Luckily there are enough good novels from past generations to keep me occupied the rest of my life.ics have hailed this style as \\"the next thing in fiction.\\" I'm convinced they're simply trying to be the first critic to have caught the New Wave. When I was a child, my mother always complained that my hair looked \\"like it was combed with an egg-beater.\\" I so wanted to feel long, swinging hair on my shoulders that I pinned scarves to my head. My mother always threatened that if I didn't take better care of my hair; i.e.,control its unruliness, she would cut it off. And just before my 3rd grade field trip to take a boat cruise in the harbor, that is exactly what she did! I was humiliated to discover that with the ocean's humidity I looked like a poodle! In my teens I slept on rollers as big as orange juice cans and even subjected my hair to a home-straightening kit, which ruined it and caused it to break off and thin. In my 20's I learned that if I kept my hair cut really short and used a big round brush to blow-dry it, my hair would be smoother. But the results never were exactly as I'd hoped and never lasted. Give me a rainstorm, fog, sweatiness, or too much handling, and it would all come undone. For years I wouldn't allow my husband to touch my hair for fear that my hair would revert to its natural state. I was living in the Desert Southwest and was still dutifully shampooing my hair every morning to remove the ton of hair products I loaded onto it the previous day and then blow-dying it before I went to work. The result of my efforts with the diffuser was that my hair was dry and brittle and frizzy. When I would unexpectedly catch a glimpse of myself in a mirror or window Iwould think, \\"who's that!\\"<br /><br />Massey's book was a revelation from the beginning and I actually read it through in one sitting. The next day I followed her suggestions and was amazed at how much curl I actually had in my hair, even short. The 2nd day I read the labels on my haircare products and tossed out the ones with the drying ingredients, and even bravely disposed of my round styling brushes. Once I embraced my curliness, I decided that I really needed to grow it out some since without blow-drying, it looked really short! Each day my hair seemed to get shinier and by following the styling advice in Massey's book, my technique improved too. I haven't gotten a single negative comment or look from my friends and family. (Indeed, they are relieved that I am no longer so hair-crzed and dissatisfied with its appearance.) But best of all, I have come to terms with my curls and even discovered that I like the way they look and I am proud of them. I am so glad that I discovered \\"Curly Girl: the Handbook\\" that day in the library. I wish there had been a book like it years ago when I was really young. It has changed my life for the better.

PULSE was originally released in June of 95 and sold over 2 million copies in only one month.  It debuted in the number one position due to preorders and I purchased one of the original CD's when record stores still existed.  It was released in a box with a pulsing (blinking) red light on the spine and the two CD's were housed inside both covers of a book which slipped in and out of the case nicely.  A very nice production job on the marketing and if you can lay your hands on one of these now collectibles you will find the battery is easily replaceable and the album will still blink (unless someone let the battery sit inside and corrode horribly).  Mine actually lasted over three years without needing initial replacing!  I think it averages about 2 years on a new battery each time and it is still blinking away!  Ah  but you want to hear about the music!  I don't know what the latest CD release is housed in but I can tell you that even if the latest release is not \\"re-mastered\\" worry not.  PULSE was one of the best engineered live albums and one of the best 90's releases period!  The clarity, the definition, the balance of loudness (too many remasters end up bottoming out horribly), and the audience amplitude are all meticulously produced in order to give the listener a wonderful live-capture feeling (without the swell of audience drowning out the music) while keeping the virtuosity of Pink Floyd right up front and separated into gorgeous stereo definition.  Even on a surround system where you set your amplifier to \\"concert surround\\" settings, the stereo will translate beautifully into pseudo-surround.  Gilmour's guitar as always is one of, if not THE, greatest guitar sounds in the world and he stretches his studio solos out here without becoming repetitive and boring.  His notes snatch glimpses of glory and emote right up there on the Carlos Santana level.  The Floyd, even without Waters, are also, as usual, perfect!  Pink Floyd have always been known as perfectionists.  In the old days, they marked the floor up with little x's as designated places to stand whether you were the saxophonist, a backup singer, or David yourself, and God-forbid you stray from your mark or the band would just go crazy.  The visual effects were note-for-note tied to the music with seldom to never an error.  Any concert of theirs you attended was an event worth remembering and cherishing.  The music on both discs here, already reviewed ad infinitum on these pages, is just plain glorious!  The Dark Side Of The Moon is a very nice treat with some embellishments that add to and do not detract from the original.  One note here, no malice to the glorious original of Clare Torry, but huge kudos have to be sent out to the astounding vocals of Sam Brown on Great Gig In The Sky! I honestly love this version more than the original just because of her solo.  If you are unfamiliar with Sam Brown, she is mainly a European act, member of Jools Holland (original founding member of Squeeze) band and daughter of English rock and roller Joe Brown, all who were very close friends to George Harrison.  Jools and Sam perform Harrison's last recording Horse To Water, on The Concert For George.  Sam's vocals on that piece rival the best sounds to ever come from the lips of Aretha Franklin, that's how good a singer she is, and here, Pink Floyd let her loose on one of the craziest but greatest songs to ever come from their archives.  Her work on backing is all over both of these CD's and one has to acknowledge that the backing vocals on PULSE are some of the finest ever produced on a Floyd album.  As for the rest of the music, just read more reviews and look at the overall ranking and that should tell you whether or not you would be wasting your money here.  Not!  This is one of the best live recordings ever made, yes!  Let's put it simply:<br /><br />The album starts moody with an incredible opening salvo of Shine On You Crazy Diamond featuring all the best parts and while the audience is reeling from that, the spacey dissonance and drums of Astronomy Domine rendered in the Ummagumma style (with the sad exception that the flying saucer sound-effect is missing as well as the quiet organ solo in the middle, this is the only bad mark I can give the entire PULSE set).  From there we get the best extended versions of the best of Momentary Lapse Of Reason, Division Bell, Gilmour's best songs from The Wall, a heart-felt rendering of Wish You Were Here, and all of Dark Side Of The Moon from heartbeat and laughter to heartbeat fade-out, there is no DARK side of the moon, really...  Now what more could a Pink Floyd fan ask for?  And if you are a novice looking for a good selection of Floyd, do not let the word \\"live\\" scare you away! 0375842209|5.0|\\"I have hated the words and I have loved them, and I hope I have made them right.\\"  - p. 528    There are some books that you read and enjoy and perhaps recommend to a friend. There are some that quickly pass a few hours and leave your head nearly as fast. Then there are those that haunt you, nestling themselves deep within you, allowing you to reflect on their brilliance and potency.    Needless to say, Markus Zusak's \\"The Book Thief\\" falls into that third category.    This is a story told by Death (quite literally - it is the narrator) about a girl named Liesel Memminger. In 1939, she is dropped off in the town of Molching, near Munich, to live with foster parents, Hans and Rosa Hubermann. Her brother had died on the train journey to the town, and she's not entirely sure why she has to be separated from her mother. But it is this event that truly starts Liesel's story. For when her brother died, she stole her first book - \\"The Grave Digger's Handbook\\". Her new papa finds it an odd choice for reading material until he realizes that, though she wants to more than anything, Liesel cannot read. When she wakes up in the night from nightmares of her brother dying, Hans takes his foster daughter to the basement to paint words on the walls. This, plus a lot of hard work, changes Liesel's world.    Rudy Steiner is the boy next door. One of many siblings, he has a reputation in the neighborhood for his stint a few years earlier when he painted himself black and ran around the track in town pretending to be Jesse Owens. From the beginning, Rudy and Liesel become close friends, playing soccer together in the street, attending classes together at school, and finding trouble to get into. However, their world is changing fast. Germany has invaded Poland, and the war quickly finds its way to Molching.    \\"The Book Thief\\" is the story of one girl's life from 1939 to 1943. To the outside world, she is quite unremarkable - just a pre-teen who lives an ordinary life in Germany. Her concerns aren't for the state of the world, but like many children, she is far wiser than many adults. Liesel is confronted by poverty, racism, the Holocaust, crime, unemployment, rationing, social standing, Nazism, and many other events that shape her into the person she has become. And, unlike other YA books that touch on World War II, this book is told with both an innocence and a sagacity that is completely unique. It is descriptive, full of color and life and makes you fall in love with its characters before you've even realized it. It is completely unlike anything I have ever come across before, and while it is a quick read, it is meant to be savored, like any masterpiece.

Perhaps the Jefferson Airplane's Grace Slick was the closest thing we had of being a powerful female presence (Janis was a powerful singer but obviously had issues that weakened her).<br /><br />\\"Horses\\" is a seminal document of the `70's. Opening with the infamous line \\"Jesus died for somebody's sins but not mine\\" from \\"Gloria: In Excelsis Deo\\" which in its punk sacrilege excited me to no end as a lapsed-Catholic, it starts off languidly and builds from there courtesy of Patti's excellent band with a core of guitarist and original coconspirator Lenny Kaye, keyboard player Richard Sohl, guitarist Ivan Kral and drummer Jay Dee Daugherty with help from guests Tom Verlaine (one of Television's guitarists), Alan Lanier (from Blue Oyster Cult and Patti's paramour at the time) and producer John Cale from the Velvet Undergound which was a huge influence on Patti but was reportedly difficult to work with.<br /><br />Thieves, lost boys, extraterrestrial activity, altered consciousness and death are the main themes that permeate \\"Horses\\". Patti switches genders back and forth throughout \\"Gloria\\" and the rest of the album with dramatic effect. Her cabaret background allows her to do this fluently and naturally. After \\"Gloria: In Excelsis Deo\\", the gears switch to the pseudo-reggae of \\"Redondo Beach\\" in which Patti sings of \\"sweet suicide\\", which could be interpreted as one of many references to her heroes Jimi Hendrix, Janis Joplin and especially Jim Morrison. In the jazz-inflected, transcendent \\"Birdland\\" Patti speaks/sings about a boy's fourth encounter with an alien (among other things). This is also one of the many songs that concerns itself with alternate forms of consciousness. The lyrics alone are enough to transport one into another state of mind with the music perfectly capturing a feeling of disorientation, especially the spacey, effects-laden guitars. \\"Free Money\\" rocks wildly, a poor person's dream possibly inspired by Patti's background and the financial problems she and friend/lover Robert Mapplethorpe experienced before they became famous. \\"Kimberly\\", named after her sister, is  a funky little song featuring the great lines \\"I feel like some misplaced Joan of Arc\\" and \\"bats with their baby vein faces\\". With its splitting skies and colliding planets, it seems to be a meditation on the creative and destructive forces inherent in this life. For additional sonic drama, Patti actually pounds rhythmically on her chest while singing passages of the triumphant \\"Break It Up\\" with the guitar echoing the vocals on the chorus beautifully. Horses, horses, horses- you can almost hear them galloping on \\"Land\\", a suite that leads off with \\"horses\\", with alter ego Johnny dipping into the sea of possibilities and death; segues into \\"land of a thousand dances\\" with multiple Pattis speak/singing over one another hypnotically, creating another disorienting effect on the listener. The suite ends with \\"la mer (de)\\", a delightfully playful pun of `murder'. The album ends with the funereal \\"Elegie\\", a lovely, melancholy farewell to Patti's (and my) tragic rock heroes.<br /><br />I bought Ms. Smith's next couple of albums and though there were moments of inspiration on these, none would ever come close to this piece of unique magic- \\"three chords merged with the power of the word\\" is how she put it in her recent, exquisitely written memoir \\"Just Kids\\". B00004VWA7|5.0|Jaco Pastorius' first solo LP is literally mindblowing. He starts by tugging on Charlie Parker's cape by covering Donna Lee, a Miles tune recorded by Bird when Miles was in his band. Then he brings in Sam and Dave to sing on an r&b number, Come On Come Over, in which the speed and precision of his 16th notes is remarkable. He starts \\"Continuum\\" with a triple-stop harmonic, just because he can, and Jaco wannabes (me included) have been playing this song continuously for the last 35 years. \\"A Portrait of Tracy\\" is a similar setpiece for bassists, a drop dead gorgeous piece (written for his first wife) which includes more harmonics than you thought were humanly possible. This CD and his other studio solo effort (Word of Mouth) are jaw droppers. Jaco died young so he didn't get to make much music, but his genius burned brightly (perhaps a little too brightly) for about 10 years. He gigged with Pat Metheny, Joni Mitchell, and Weather Report, which cut into the time he had to make his own records. Shamefully, Jaco's third solo album, Holiday for Pans, has never been released (though it has been bootlegged). Jaco's genius encompassed composition and arranging, and he produced a record which featured these two facets of his musicality-- but not a note of his bass playing. The suits thought he was nuts, but it wasn't madness. It was genius. \\"Jaco Pastorius\\" remains in Amazon's top 20 bestsellers for fusion jazz-- 35 years after it was released. Unfortunately Jaco did not get much of the financial reward. When he died his estate came to $600. In an ironic touch for a man who spent his last year homeless, living in a park in Fort Lauderdale, Fort Lauderdale now has a park named after him, with two outsize wall murals of the great maestro. Jaco lives!

B000F3A7LE|5.0|Robert Fripp's first solo album after King Crimson split up is nothing short of surprisingly marvelous.  I wasn't expecting this at all, but the numerous amount of positive reviews here on amazon encouraged me to check it out.    \\"You Burn Me Up I'm a Cigarette\\" is a fast-paced 50's style rocker radically updated with late 70's technology and given a Talking Heads-like feel.  It's extremely heavy during certain parts, and just awesome.  \\"Breathless\\" is a continuation of the \\"Red\\" theme from King Crimson's Red album, but of course -just like the previous track- updated to fit the (then) modern times.  It's not nearly as raw or aggressive as the King Crimson song but it's definitely good nonetheless.    \\"Disengage\\" proves that Peter Hammill can be absolutely *insane* with his vocal range.  He positively screams and screeches and sends chills through my body with this performance, and I actually detect small signs of Rob Halford mannerisms from time to time.  You know, the lead singer of Judas Priest.  Perhaps a total coincidence.  I was never a huge fan of Daryl Hall even though my parents loved Hall & Oates to pieces, but his singing style on \\"North Star\\" is drastically different from any Hall & Oates stuff I'm familiar with.  He really expands his range while some beautifully performed guitar trickery is taking place in the background.  Subtle, but it's there.      \\"Chicago\\" contains a beautifully sung vocal melody courtesy of Peter Hammill though I wish it were longer because 2 minutes isn't enough to accomplish what could have been a REALLY splendid and meaningful song.  \\"NY3\\" is some chaotic mess that never does anything in particular, and \\"Mary\\" features sentimental female vocals from Terre Roche.  This song in particular is a drastic departure from the rest of the album because it's so soft and quiet.  You'll notice this right away.  It's still really good though, make no mistake about it!      The title song is quite the strange one.  Listen carefully to the trickery in the background.  Sound familiar?  Yeah, it's the final section of \\"Formentera Lady\\" (the King Crimson song) repeating that same section but in a modern kind of way.  What's especially appealing about this song is the female singers style and the way she screams \\"EXPOOOOOSUUUUUURE\\" all the way through.  In fact at the end she just lets loose completely and does nothing BUT scream.  The beat is groovy, so it's definitely unique.  \\"Haaden Two\\" sounds like more King Crimson-like material.    \\"Urban Landscape\\" sounds stolen from the Eno/Fripp album Evening Star (particularly side two) and \\"I May Not Have Had Enough of Me but I've Had Enough of Typing Out This Long Song Title\\" includes another performance by Peter Hammill based on top of a fast-paced almost punk-like guitar riff, and he's constantly trading vocals with Terre Roche.  For some reason it doesn't sound natural at all and it's overlong by about a minute, but I like it nonetheless.      \\"Water Music I\\" is really nothing but a repetitive guitar solo and it's a lot like something from the Eno/Fripp album Evening Star.  The lyrics are funny because they're about the world freezing in 40 years time.  Yeah right!  More like just the opposite.  \\"Here Comes the Flood\\" has some amazingly compassionate vocals courtesy of Peter Gabriel.  It reminds me of a Genesis song, for sure.  \\"Water Music 2\\" is more repetition that neither excites or irritates me.    Interesting enough, this albums main strength is NOT that it's a Robert Fripp solo album, but rather the diversity that's featured -perhaps unintentionally- because of all the popular guest musicians.  These guests are the reason Exposure is so unusually fascinating and diverse.  I highly recommend it.

The writing style of Plath is beautiful throughout, and what strikes me is how current Esther and her situations seem today.  I felt at the end of the novel as if the book could have been released this past week, it's that true to life. Esther is so much like many people I've met, and she faces the same turmoil that many young people today still face. I could not sleep at night if I were to offer any fewer than 5 stars to this book. 0671510991|5.0|Kissinger's ambitious goal is to provide a review of diplomatic thought, theory, practice and result, as conducted primarily in Europe (or by European/Western powers) from the time of Richelieu through to the time of his writing (1993).  A book of erudition, knowledge, discernment, and powerful analysis, Diplomacy fully succeeds in allowing the reader to more fully understand the underlying currents of national grand strategy over a period of nearly 400 years.    Kissinger begins with the development of the concept of the nation-state, and the strategic goals of France (especially a group of splintered and weak statelets in the German area on France's eastern border).  Other countries, especially Britain, will react to these positions; which reactions foster further reactions, and so forth, more or less to the present day.    One recurrent theme throughout the book is the issue of Germany.  The German states, prior to unification, provided endless opportunities for other countries to attempt to gain dominance in central Europe at their expense; France first, but soon to be followed by Austria, which encouraged others (Britain) to repeatedly intervene in order to assure that no one country would in fact gain mastery of Europe.  But Germany once united has, it is safe to say, not necessarily been a stabilizing influence either.      Kissinger's tracking of European thought in grand strategy is fascinating, and his respect and admiration for some key figures - Richelieu, Bismarck, Talleyrand, Theodore Roosevelt - is obvious and well explained.  But there are other ways of perceiving and setting strategy, of setting goals, and using diplomacy to reach them - with the dawn of the 20th century, we are on the cusp of the United States making itself felt on a global scale.  And the United States has acted very differently from the way Europe had conducted itself for the previous 300 years.    America's self-appointed role as global crusader has been remarked upon and analyzed before, but Kissinger's detailed examination is riveting (and sometimes shocking).  One cannot understand current events without understanding the path America's leaders, both Democrat and Republican, have taken; the sole exception since Teddy Roosevelt having been Richard Nixon.      Kissinger thus takes us from crisis point to treaty to myriad personalities on the world stage; the sweep is as grand as could be.  Where it tends to slow down is once we are in the Cold War, and we deal with Berlin, with Korea, Vietnam (three longer chapters), disarmament, detente, the opening of China and Nixon's triangular diplomacy, and the ending of the Cold War.  Having read many of Kissinger's other works previously, including his massive three volume memoirs of the Nixon/Ford years, a good bit of this was already overly familiar (if given a different angle of approach).      This reader found the first two thirds of the book to be thoroughly engrossing, deeply engaging without being terribly technical, superbly written, fair and punctilious in its characterizations of attitudes and personalities admired or not by the author, and superb in execution.  The final third, covering a much shorter period in which is could be said that Kissinger himself has something of a point to prove (and re-prove), could be seen to drag somewhat, and I would imagine that many contemporaries would be more inclined to disagree in some fundamental ways with the arguments advanced (though this reader felt all logically argued and justifiable).    Kissinger's work is an outstanding book to read covering history in a way not often addressed from the angle of national policy in theory and practice.

As for the ultraendurance piece, the verdict is still out on that (as of this writing Ferris either still hasn't run the 50 mile race he was training for or hasn't posted anything about it.)  It feels like it is working and the workouts are very short (between 20 to 30 minutes per session, not including practice time on the track or road) but as any athlete will tell you, endurance takes more than a couple of months to develop (regardless of whether you use high intensity or high volume.)  In my opinion, experience tells me that the advice given in this book (for endurance) will get you part of the way there with far fewer hours and much quicker than using conventional methods, but ultimately to get to an elite level will require longer sessions (but probably not as many as was once thought in the past.)    Overall, I found the book to be very motivating and fun to read due to the various anecdotes that Ferris shares with us.  If nothing else, his words should be enough to motivate you to go after higher levels of athletic performance, or to scare you enough to get off your butt and pay attention to your health.

B0056BMK6K|3.0|The most important thing to remember is that this is a how to book, essentially and even the most detailed and sincere how to books offer more hope than substance. My initial reaction to the book was the same as with all the other how to books I've bought (and there are many) which was to start punching the air, shouting yesss, and making posts saying I'd found the best thing ever and had started preparing for author superstardom. Couple of weeks later, you guessed it, the book is sitting on the lonely shelf with all the other how to books that promised me a quick fix but never delivered. Well, it would be if it had been a real book, I bought the kindle version. And the reason it will probably stay there? I bought another book, similar promise, I mean premise, sell gazillions of books using only a piece of string, some social media and one small blog. Only problem is that this book said both the same (good news bad news, but more of that later) and something different (cue confusion). The similarities went like this - put on your most sincere smile, befriend folk on twitter, facebook etc, be sure to be a really sincere friend, show interest in them and their posts, work etc, then sell them your book. Or hope that they'll somehow find your book, which is the same thing. Where they differed was that Locke says blog infrequently, the second book said no less than three times a week or you're wasting your time. At this point I was both alarmed and confused. Alarmed because very soon the whole of the indie book community is going to look like a Miss Author Pageant, with everyone smiling earnestly, trying to look interested in other people, while at the same time calculating how long they'll have to listen before mentioning their book. And we'll all be gushing over kittens and puppies, celebrities and starving kids in africa in our blogs in an effort to look concerned, altruistic, and hopefully get some good SEO hits so we can sell people our books. It's going to be a summer of love that has no end (until you buy my book) after which I promise to stop smiling and stop pretending to be your friend.     I'm giving the book three stars because I enjoyed the read and it reminded me that I need to promote. Taking off two stars because I'm blaming it (and others) in advance for making authors believe that friendship is synonymous with marketing. Yes, the two aren't mutually exclusive, but my friends don't send me chatty emails full of buy links. I don't do that to my friends, either. By all means be nice to people, show an interest in their posts, but do it like you really mean it, not just because you want them to buy your book. It's a system that works brilliantly if a few people are doing it. Once everyone starts doing it the smiles will become a tad fixed and it will lose effectiveness. So yes, I recommend the read, but I'd say think hard and long before trying the system yourselves. It will only work if you buy into the principles. It's not a right or a wrong system, it's a great system that worked wonderfully for a man with a brilliant marketing mind. If you think like a marketing department, it will probably work for you. If you don't, it probably won't. And, if like me you're addicted to the quick fix lure of the self help book, it's probably already languishing in your kindle archives, or sitting lonely on the shelf.

B003Y5H542|4.0|Rango is a brilliantly designed film. It's a western, starring desert animals that look every inch the part. It is a triumph of character design, drawing on a vastly underutilized subset of the animal kingdom. The casting is note perfect. Toads, possums, lizards, tortoises, and the deadliest rattlesnake gunslinger this side of the Rio Grande. Desert animals tend to be scaly, bumpy, and scruffy, and they seem so at home in the mining colony of Dirt, it makes other talking creature movies look like they were designed by plush animal companies. The irony is that if they ever made toy action figures of those mariachi owls, I would buy mine in a heart beat.<br /><br />The story is rather simple. It's the same plot from A Bug's Life, which is the same plot as The Brave Little Tailor. A newcomer (recently escaped lizard Rango) is mistaken for a hero to help solve the town's big problem. in this case, it's a lack of water. Had the movie simply stuck with this tried and true narrative, it could have become one of the all time greats.<br /><br />Instead, the filmmakers seemed determined to load it down with weird quirky bits that look like they belong in a Wes Anderson film. Rango's lizard love interest has a brain glitch that causes her to space out for minutes on end. There's a bizarre homage to Eastwood's Man With No Name, who drives around in a golf cart. The townsfolk line up to perform a strange rain dance every Wednesday. Rango himself spends the first several minutes of the movie putting on an odd one-man show before his aquarium is tossed out the window. These episodes add nothing to film, and only serve to jar us out of the expertly realized Wild West.<br /><br />The bits that work best are based on those time-honored motifs. Rango leading his posse out into desert in search of bank robbers. A runaway wagon chase, with a hundred bats in pursuit. Facing down that slimy rattlesnake at high noon. Too bad that storytellers want to keep busting up these scenes with walking cactus spirits and mystic roadkill. Rango is definitely a beautiful film to watch. It just could have used some tighter focus.stic roadkill. Rango is definitely a beautiful film to watch. It just could have used some tighter focus.

B000QCS8TW|4.0|I suppose it's not unusual for fantasy books to be long or slow-starting, but A Game of Thrones felt longer and slow-starting-er than most. Once it gets going, it turns into a gripping tale of kingly bloodlines, monsters from the woods, and royals in exile.<br /><br />Surprisingly, the political intrigue takes center stage, departing from the genre's usual focus on magic spells and epic battle sequences. The thoughts and motivations of the characters call to mind the moral conflicts of Orson Scott Card's work, and make for a far more interesting read than the usual mage-fighting-a-dragon. Yet it's not without its action sequences. Or its brutality. Early on, one character -- a young child -- is paralyzed from a high fall. Other characters are raped, beheaded, or similarly made uncomfortable. Several wolf puppies are also killed, which is somehow even more awful. The novel features more than its fair share of sex, giving it a grittier feel than its peers.<br /><br />A Game of Thrones is sometimes its own worst enemy. The sheer volume of characters puts biblical genealogies to shame. If by the end of the first reading you've got a good feel for half of the major and minor players, consider yourself lucky. So many lords and squires and young ranger boys traipse through the pages, it takes a few paragraphs to figure out which names are the good guys, the bad guys, the wolf pups, and the cities.<br /><br />Like all Book One's of a fantasy series, this one ends just when you've decided you'd like to know the answers to some of the questions. I presume Book 2 (and 3 and 4) will start to resolve this Game. But I have to say, it made me want to keep reading, so I think it's done its job.

0061784788|2.0|I am an avid reader, especially of the thriller genre, and there are a handful of authors who's work I actively look forward to and seek to get my hands on as quickly as possible, even if it means buying and Advanced Reader's Copy on e-bay. For me,  James Rollins is one of those authors. In this instance, I exercised the patience to borrow it from the local library (but I got on the wait list very, very early). I was anxious to read this novel because it features great characters from past Sigma force works like Painter Crowe, Gray Pierce, Monk, Kowalski, and Seichan. Good start. However, overall, I found the story line to be rather dull. The aging Sigma crew came off as old, tired and ready for retirement. Yet, against all odds, they still managed to save the world from imminent destruction. By the end of the novel, it is clear that one of them is ready to retire, one or two of them SHOULD retire, and the operation needs \\"new blood\\" in a big way if it is to continue. The plot intermingles native american lore with secrets kept by some of America's founding fathers, most notably Thomas Jefferson, and the threat posed by so-called \\"nanotechnology\\". The author's opinion that nanotechnology has ancient roots is, to me, difficult to swallow. This conjecture was not proven to my satisfaction in \\"The Devil Colony\\". Just because we don't understand how an ancient sword was manufactured, and even if microscopic analysis of the material revealed the presence of \\"nano\\" strands of something, this does not necessarily mean that the creators of that sword understood the concept of nanotechnology. In fact, the term nanotechnology itself is nebulous. Basically, the \\"nano world\\" is the level of individual molecules. We have very few tools to observe the nano world. One is high powered electron microscopy, which can generate images of the \\"nano world\\" at its upper end--that is, molecules that are multiple nanometers in length. The other is X-ray diffraction of crystalline molecules, which can image, via electron density, atomic resolution images of molecules at a accuracy of 0.1 namometers or even slightly better. A strand of DNA is part of the nano world. And cells carry out nano reactions involving DNA all the time. So nature uses nanotechnology all the time. So how do humans use it? So far, mostly it is done by chemical rather than biological reactions. One group recently produced DNA molecules that resembled a \\"smiley face\\". Eventually, molecular polymers may revolutionalize computer science, body armor, and other things mentioned by Rollins. But at present, this science is in its infancy, and no one understands its potential. What is totally off the wall in \\"The Devil Colony\\" is the idea that ancient people not only understood the advanced concept of nanotechnology (they didn't even know what a molecule was!), but were somehow also able to harness its potential to create a dangerous substance with the potential to destroy the world. I ask you: how could they have done this (maybe it came from an advanced alien culture?), and why? Where did the \\"nanonests\\" described in the novel originate from? This is not explained. And what was their purpose? Also not explained. There are additional scientific questions that the book raises. For instance, if these nanonests, presumably consisting of nanobots, are breaking down matter into something that resembles dust, then where are they getting the energy to do so and where is the energy that is dissipated by breaking matter into smaller (unspecified) entities dissipated? E=mc(2). When mass is destroyed, energy must be produced. One would think this energy would be liberated in the form of heat, which, according to the novel, would destroy the nanonests. So how exactly does that work? It doesn't. At least not for this reader/scientist. \\"The Devil Colony\\" is a slow read for a Rollins novel, and by the end I was skimming the pages and hoping it would all be over soon. This is a far cry from previous recent novels by the same author, which this reader/scientist found to be more scientifically plausible and more suspenseful. I'm sorry to say that I was disappointed by this latest Sigma Force novel. And I think it may be time to put the Sigma Force to rest. I have focused mainly on the scientific aspects of this novel but the character development and general story line were also weaker than what I have come to expect from Rollins. This novel ended up being an unsatisfactory meld between a by now run-of-the-mill type Dan Brown decryption plot and the Michael Crichton novel \\"Prey\\". However, Crichton did a better job making the science of nanobots seem credible.nt novels by the same author, which this reader/scientist found to be more scientifically plausible and more suspenseful. I'm sorry to say that I was disappointed by this latest Sigma Force novel. And I think it may be time to put the Sigma Force to rest. I have focused mainly on the scientific aspects of this novel but the character development and general story line were also weaker than what I have come to expect from Rollins. This novel ended up being an unsatisfactory meld between a by now run-of-the-mill type Dan Brown decryption plot and the Michael Crichton novel \\"Prey\\". B0051DSAP8|5.0|I'd never heard of Powerwolf until a few weeks ago, but I haven't been this excited about a new band discovery in years. Several songs feature melodies and arias lifted straight out of Catholic mass, and this liturgical melodrama mixed with bombastic metal makes for a skin-crawling audio experience.  Close your eyes and you can just imagine this band blasting through the naves of Notre Dame cathedral or Westminster Abbey.  Attila Dorn's vocals are monstrous and avoid the pitfall of many power metal vocalists to remain at a constant stratospheric range.  He doesn't hit falsetto notes, but his voice soars over the music and fills the sonic space like an orchestra.  Speaking of orchestra, the bonus edition of this album has a companion CD featuring classical reinterpretations of Powerwolf songs by a Russian filmscore composer.  Epic beyond imagination.<br /><br />The lyrical content is the only weak point in my opinion, but that's just a matter of taste.  With music this massive, I would expect the lyrics to be about end of days battles between angels and demons and whatnot, rather than goth-punk poetry about creative ways to die and werewolves and supernatural creepiness.  But this has been Powerwolf's modus operandi since their creation, so they can rock out however they want.<br /><br />Simply put, theological differences aside, Powerwolf would be the result if the Pope commissioned a metal band to make the most reverent, majestic, worshipful metal music ever created. B004TWOXIU|5.0|La Tosca, a play written by the French playwright Sardou, was Produced in Paris in 1887. In 1889 Puccini saw the play,which he liked. Eventually, it became the opera we know. The original play had five acts and 23 characters. Puccini's opera has nine characters and is in three acts. He was only interested in the interaction between Scarpia,Tosca and Cavaradossi. The opera is set in June 1800,the time of the battle at Marengo, where Napoleon defeated the Austrian forces. The Libretto was written by Giuseppe Giacosa and Luigi Illica. The two worked as a team and were responsible for La Boheme, Tosca and Madame Butterfly. Illica made the prose sketch of the work and Giacosa then versified and polished it,with Puccini's input. Tosca's premiere  was in 1900 in Rome.    This version of Tosca sounds and looks like a play with singing and music. Set in the Period of 1800,the staging and costumes which are stunning are traditional. The centre piece is of the head ,shoulders and hand with a sword of Emperor Constantine,who first made Christianity a state religion.The symbolism shows the power of the Church and the state. In the Church service  the monks carry the cross and so forth.Cannons are used. I hope your neighbours are tolerant. In the second act, Scarpias desk is shown. The third act we are on the roof of the prison. This opera is staged in the huge open air arena at Verona,with what I call traditional minimalism sets. The orchestra and chorus of the Arena of Verona are conducted by Daniel Oren, passionate and fast tempo when required. Fiorenza Cedolins who is a singer actress, brings out the human character of Tosca,as opposed to the usual over the top rendition of the part.  Marcelo Alvarez is Cavaradossi. Scarpia sung by Ruggero Raimondi, is not the usual evil  police chief, but subtle,charming,sly,and ruthless. What is amazing is that all three are believable. On such a huge stage and theatre,this opera seems intimate. Usually it doesnt. Probably, because Raimondiand Cedolins are Italian, this makes a difference. All the parts fit the whole and works. As I stated it seems like a play, not a opera. The blu ray is amazing,probably one of my best. Worldwide.
